    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span id="freeTextContainer9509895746866118877">A meaningful play based on the Scopes Monkey Trial of 1925, in which a Tennessee teacher was tried for teaching evolution.   <span id="freeText9509895746866118877" style="display:none">A meaningful play based on the Scopes Monkey Trial of 1925, in which a Tennessee teacher was tried for teaching evolution. The accused was a slight, frightened man who'd deliberately broken the law. His trial was a Roman circus, the chief gladiators being the two great legal giants of the century. Locked in mortal combat, they bellowed &amp; roared imprecations &amp; abuse. The spectators sat uneasily in the sweltering heat with murder in their hearts, barely restraining themselves. America's freedom was at stake.</span>

<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/38731207-phil"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1420364528i/38731207._UY100_CR5,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/38731207-phil">Phil</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    Brady dies in the end because: His faith was shaken. The law was on his side, and God was on his side. Even though he won the case, he knew the trial <a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    Brady dies in the end because: His faith was shaken. The law was on his side, and God was on his side. Even though he won the case, he knew the trial wasn't about that. The trial was about the sanctity of keeping religion in schools. (Not putting in my opinion, just stating how Brady saw it). He didn't understand how The Bible could be made a mockery of. Each instance tore him apart inside until the end when, even though, legally, he won; he knew he had really lost. He died a broken man.<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>
</div>

<div id="bookReviews">
  




  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1761262749" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1761262749" />
    <a title="Nandakishore Varma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6237864-nandakishore-varma"><img alt="Nandakishore Varma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583648733p2/6237864.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1761262749?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nandakishore Varma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nandakishore Varma" href="/user/show/6237864-nandakishore-varma">Nandakishore Varma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780553228939" href="/book/show/861939.Inherit_the_Wind">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1761262749" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6840765742285063365">The "Scopes Monkey Trial"(1925) is perhaps the most famous trial in American history where a young teacher, John Scopes, was accused of teaching evolution in a school in the small town of Dayton, Tennessee in violation of Tennessee's "Butler Act" which prohibited teaching evolution in any state-funded school. According to all reliable sources, the trial was a put-up job by some prominent locals to garner some publicity for the town, knowing fully well that the ACLU had offered to take up the def</span>
  <span id="freeText6840765742285063365" style="display:none">The "Scopes Monkey Trial"(1925) is perhaps the most famous trial in American history where a young teacher, John Scopes, was accused of teaching evolution in a school in the small town of Dayton, Tennessee in violation of Tennessee's "Butler Act" which prohibited teaching evolution in any state-funded school. According to all reliable sources, the trial was a put-up job by some prominent locals to garner some publicity for the town, knowing fully well that the ACLU had offered to take up the defense of anyone accused under this law. John Scopes was a willing victim who was not sure at all he had taught evolution: the battle was actually between fundamentalists and modernists within Christianity. Even though the result was a foregone conclusion, the trial accomplished its mission to bring science vs religion debate into the mainstream. <br /><br />In this play, however, John Scopes has been transformed into Giles Bates, an idealistic young teacher who has been victimised for standing on the side of scientific truth. The whole town is out for his blood and he is really facing the ruin of his career. His love interest Rachel is the daughter of Brown, the town's fiery preacher: thus providing the appropriate "star-crossed lover" motif. And battle here is for free speech in America, fought out on the courtroom floor between Brady, the conservative counsel for the prosecution and Drummond, the agnostic barrister for the defence - two legal Goliaths.<br /><br />The authors seem to have written this play as a reaction to McCarthyism, and they have made it clear that even though it is based on the Scopes trial, it is not historical. However, it does follow the actual course of events to great extent except for stretching the dramatic elements. But it is not as a historical record that one should view this, but rather as an impassioned plea for open thought and free speech - and in that, they have won.<br /><br />The opening scene between the children sets the tone - when little Howard says that he came from a worm which crawled out of the sea, we can see that the gates to free thought has been opened in at least one tender mind. As the play progresses and the action alternates between the town and the courthouse, the audience can see a people being slowly transformed: they still believe in the Bible, yet they can now accept someone else can have a different line of thought. The cross-examination of Brady as a witness for the defense (this really happened, BTW!) by Drummond is the heart of the play - the literal interpretation of scripture is here laid bare as the foolish exercise it is. The real target of the drama is the audience, and this sequence is bound to set at least some minds in the theatre thinking.<br /><br />I think this is one play which should be revived - and not only in America. With religious bigots stepping up their attack on secular elements across the world (sometime even resorting to physical violence), we should remind ourselves that the right to free speech has been won only with great effort. It is very easy to lose it: those of us who value it, should fight tooth and nail.<br /><br />And drama is one of the strongest media.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6840765742285063365" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3007974780" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3007974780" />
    <a title="Dan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10770371-dan"><img alt="Dan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1540580928p2/10770371.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3007974780?book_show_action=true">Oct 12, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan" href="/user/show/10770371-dan">Dan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10770371-dan?shelf=pulitzer-drama">pulitzer-drama</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3007974780" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4594980018694740150">Inherit the Wind<br/><br/><b>Howard: What’re yuh skeered of? You was a worm once.<br/><br/>Melinda: I wasn’t neither!<br/><br/>Howard: You was so! When the whole world was covered with water, there was nuthin’ but worms and blobs of jelly. And you and your whole family was worms! <br/><br/>Melinda: We was not!<br/><br/>Howard: Blobs of jelly, then.<br/><br/>Melinda: Howard Blair, that’s sinful talk! I’m gonna tell my pa and he’ll make you wash your mouth out with soap!<br/><br/>Howard: Ah your old man’s a monkey!</b><br/><br/>This interaction between two small children on a courth</span>
  <span id="freeText4594980018694740150" style="display:none">Inherit the Wind<br /><br /><b>Howard: What’re yuh skeered of? You was a worm once.<br /><br />Melinda: I wasn’t neither!<br /><br />Howard: You was so! When the whole world was covered with water, there was nuthin’ but worms and blobs of jelly. And you and your whole family was worms! <br /><br />Melinda: We was not!<br /><br />Howard: Blobs of jelly, then.<br /><br />Melinda: Howard Blair, that’s sinful talk! I’m gonna tell my pa and he’ll make you wash your mouth out with soap!<br /><br />Howard: Ah your old man’s a monkey!</b><br /><br />This interaction between two small children on a courthouse lawn is the beginning of one of the most famous plays/movies of the 20th century. It was inspired by the 1925 Scopes Monkey Trial in Kentucky thirty years prior.<br /><br />Inherit the Wind is a play first staged in 1955 and perhaps thousands of times since. Many people are more familiar with the wonderful screen adaptation in 1960 starring Spencer Tracy and Frederic March. <br /><br />The little boy Howard’s science teacher, Bert Cates, is about to face trial for blasphemy, straight up violating the Butler act that bans teaching evolution in the classroom.<br /><br />This three act play takes place almost entirely in the courtroom. Cates is defended by a famous lawyer, Henry Drummond, and is being prosecuted by an equally famous self-righteous lawyer, Matthew Brady. <br /><br />***** Spoiler*****<br /><br />The first act moves slowly as we are introduced to the main characters including Bert’s love interest Rachel who is ironically testifying against him in the upcoming trial. Rachel unsuccessfully pleads with Bert to denounce evolution so that the prosecution will drop the case. There is some anxiety for the prosecution as well. They are led by Brady, a character inspired by Williams Jennings Bryan. They come to learn that Drummond, the character inspired by Clarence Darrow, will be defending Bert.<br /><br />So in the second act the trial begins and we soon learn that it is going quite badly for the defense, particularly when the bible thumping judge will not allow any of the defense’s expert witnesses on evolutionary science to testify. Then in a move that strains credulity, the pompous Brady agrees to testify as an expert on creationism. The drama soon accelerates to a crescendo as Drummond takes advantage of Brady’s vanity and picks apart his creationist timelines. Some in the crowd start to laugh at Brady and he is caught off guard by the ridicule. The arguments eventually end and the jury is sent odd to deliberate. They return a few hours later with the verdict — guilty. The judge however seems to have been persuaded by Drummond’s arguments and sentences Bert to just a $100 fine. <br /><br />In the very short third act, a humiliated Brady has died of a heart attack immediately following the ruling. A big city newspaper reporter is congratulates Drummond on the victory and disparages Brady. A saddened Drummond quotes several bible verses and excoriates the reporter for not respecting diverse viewpoints. Drummond then hops on the train to head out of town.<br /><br />***** End of Spoiler ******<br /><br />4.5 stars. Highly recommended. I am rating based on the story and the reading of the play not necessarily how it was performed on stage or adapted as a movie. In a nutshell, this is a masterfully wrought drama, particularly the messaging. The introduction where the children discuss evolution is pure genius. And the last quarter of the play is equal genius. It humanely captures the the embarrassment on the stand, the verdict, the judge sentencing and the reaction to the death.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4594980018694740150" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_67544623" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/67544623" />
    <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471967140p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/67544623?book_show_action=true">Aug 15, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=drama">drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=play">play</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=courtroom">courtroom</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer67544623" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3855479193350238150">Reading this book is like witnessing a debate with the resolution that reads:<blockquote>
  <i>
    <b>Be it resolved, that men are descendants of monkeys and not created from the image and likeness of God.</b>
  </i>
</blockquote>I heard about this play when I was 8 or 9 years old. I was then in a Pacific island and it was late morning of a Good Friday and my mother told me to buy something from a store. In the Philippines, Maundy Thursday, Good Friday and Black Saturdays were the days of a year in the island when there was an eerie</span>
  <span id="freeText3855479193350238150" style="display:none">Reading this book is like witnessing a debate with the resolution that reads:<blockquote>
  <i>
    <b>Be it resolved, that men are descendants of monkeys and not created from the image and likeness of God.</b>
  </i>
</blockquote>I heard about this play when I was 8 or 9 years old. I was then in a Pacific island and it was late morning of a Good Friday and my mother told me to buy something from a store. In the Philippines, Maundy Thursday, Good Friday and Black Saturdays were the days of a year in the island when there was an eerie silence all around the town. All you could hear were mournful singing of the <i>pasyon</i>, written in a local language, being sung and it recounted the passion and resurrection of Jesus Christ. However, on that Good Friday morning, I heard the local translation of this book, <i>Inherit the Wind</i> being dramatized over the local radio of our neighbor.<br /><br />So, when I saw a copy of this book last year, I said to myself that I would also read it during the Lent. So last Good Friday, I began reading this. It was really an apt Holy Week read. <br /><br />The story is about a high school teacher who, one day in 1925, lectures on Darwin's Theory of <b>Evolution</b>: that men came from apes or from sea creatures. At that time, the Tennessee State Law prohibits the teaching of evolution as it runs contradictory to the Theory of <b>Creation</b>: that men were created from the image and likeness of God. So, the Monkey Trial happened and it was the basis of this book. I will not tell you which side won the debate, done in a courtroom, so as not to spoil your fun. However, I read that this is a required reading in high school so you might already know the ending.<br /><br />Out of curiosity, I asked this question to my 16-year old daughter who has not read this book yet. She she said that her theory was: God probably looks like a monkey. I knew she was only kidding but the first time I heard these contradicting theories, that blasphemous and ridiculous idea also came into my young silly mind. And yes, I don't like the images it creates: that God, Adam and Eve are like those characters in the movie "The Planet of the Apes." <br /><br />Personally, how do I reconcile these two theories? I put evolution in my brain and creation in my heart and never the two shall meet.<br /><br />About the title, it came from Proverbs 11:29:<i>"He that troubleth his own house shall inherit the wind, and the fool shall be servant to the wise of heart."</i> I just not sure how that verse connects to the story.<br /><br />How about you, which of the two do you believe more?</span>
  <a data-text-id="3855479193350238150" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10888076440554964883">I reread the script after seeing the play. I better appreciated that the characters are fully developed, not cardboard cutouts. (It is worth noting that this play is not about science vs. all religion, but a religious subsect that takes the Bible as literal, or completely inerrant.) Though written in the 1950s about the 1920s Scopes trial, stage timing is "not too long ago." Lawrence and Lee say "It might have been yesterday. It could be tomorrow." Indeed, the play is still fresh. Note especiall</span>
  <span id="freeText10888076440554964883" style="display:none">I reread the script after seeing the play. I better appreciated that the characters are fully developed, not cardboard cutouts. (It is worth noting that this play is not about science vs. all religion, but a religious subsect that takes the Bible as literal, or completely inerrant.) Though written in the 1950s about the 1920s Scopes trial, stage timing is "not too long ago." Lawrence and Lee say "It might have been yesterday. It could be tomorrow." Indeed, the play is still fresh. Note especially the stage direction of, "They are colorful small-town citizens, but not caricatured rubes" and Drummond's reaction to the journalist at the end of the play.<br /><br />Nice shout-out to the University of Chicago as Drummond presents his case.<br /><br />Bonus: In N. Texas, a sterling production by Tony Award-winning DTC for the next few weeks: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.theaterjones.com/ntx/reviews/20170528125619/2017-05-31/Dallas-Theater-Center/Inherit-the-Wind">http://www.theaterjones.com/ntx/revie...</a></span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonfaith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonfaith" href="/user/show/2327886-jonfaith">Jonfaith</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:

<span id="freeTextContainer8880573518693353514">Goodreads Friend Sologdin offers wonderful reviews and insights into the world of ideas. "His" recent of Inherit the Wind gave me pause; this play was likened to Ayn Rand in that the villains were very underdeveloped and stereotypical. I place the quotations around the pronoun as I don't know with any certainty whether it is indeed a male. That said, I have seen the film adaptation of Inherit the Wind at least a dozen times. I especially like Gene Kelly as Mencken. I tried to approach my reading</span>
  <span id="freeText8880573518693353514" style="display:none">Goodreads Friend Sologdin offers wonderful reviews and insights into the world of ideas. "His" recent of Inherit the Wind gave me pause; this play was likened to Ayn Rand in that the villains were very underdeveloped and stereotypical. I place the quotations around the pronoun as I don't know with any certainty whether it is indeed a male. That said, I have seen the film adaptation of Inherit the Wind at least a dozen times. I especially like Gene Kelly as Mencken. I tried to approach my reading as objectively as possible but that failed miserably as I recalled nearly line by line in the 1960 film adroitly directed by Stanley Kramer. I can see the reference to wooden antagonists but is any character in the play truly developed? Only Rachel and Ben appear either innocent or noble. The premise of the play is very simple, taking the Scopes Monkey Trial as a point of departure, rural/Southern insular hostility to outside/urban oversight is coupled with the hysteria of the McCarthy hearings and attendant paranoia. <br /><br />My chief issue with the film was the closing shot of Spencer Tracy hesitating between the Bible and Origin of Species and finally taking both. Alas this scene is in the play.</span>

<div id="review_45338208" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/45338208" />
    <a title="Melody" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/253664-melody"><img alt="Melody" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1447559563p2/253664.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/45338208?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melody" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melody" href="/user/show/253664-melody">Melody</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780345466273" href="/book/show/74104.Inherit_the_Wind">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone interested in thinking about the power of intellectual freedom</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/253664-melody?shelf=drama">drama</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer45338208" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1259854343237832663">I just finished teaching this play for the second time: I read it with students in a composition course focused on topics at the intersection of freedom and literacy. In spite of its overly familiar subject matter, the play is actually quite enjoyable in the way it uses the historical Scopes Monkey Trials as a test case for thinking through the problems of the McCarthy era, and in fact, our own. The character development is quite interesting: Hornbeck is a fabulous villain - perhaps the most com</span>
  <span id="freeText1259854343237832663" style="display:none">I just finished teaching this play for the second time: I read it with students in a composition course focused on topics at the intersection of freedom and literacy. In spite of its overly familiar subject matter, the play is actually quite enjoyable in the way it uses the historical Scopes Monkey Trials as a test case for thinking through the problems of the McCarthy era, and in fact, our own. The character development is quite interesting: Hornbeck is a fabulous villain - perhaps the most complex character in the play, and a good topic for book club discussion. The plot only intentionally develops Rachel's character, but the audience develops in its response to Drummond, in what the play slowly and subtly reveals about him; the play seems to make this move in order to illustrate the need to keep an open mind about people you might otherwise reject out of hand.<br /> <br />While the play has a tendency toward the didactic, its desire to be open, the moves it makes toward a fair presentation of its most disagreeable characters, creates space for fascinating discussion of authorial intent and the composition process. Because you can see the thought processes of the authors so transparently, it doesn't make my list of great American literary works (despite what the back of the book says), but I do think it's fun intellectual candy. It's got enough substance that you feel like you've done something productive, but not so much that you're stumped.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1259854343237832663" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_46971543" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/46971543" />
    <a title="Audrey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/999524-audrey"><img alt="Audrey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1216006885p2/999524.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/46971543?book_show_action=true">Feb 20, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Audrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Audrey" href="/user/show/999524-audrey">Audrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer46971543&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer46971543" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17866066839044761824">I have a lot of trouble grasping the metaphor of the title - Biblical language just confuses me. I think this play raises interesting ideas, was probably very revolutionary at the time it was published.... but I'm just too old and liberal and jaded to be reading it in 2009. Two things that really bothered me:<br/><br/>1) What's up with Rachel? She's such a boring little ingenue, hammering us over the head with how torn she is between doctrine and instinct, and you just want to slap her in the face and tel</span>
  <span id="freeText17866066839044761824" style="display:none">I have a lot of trouble grasping the metaphor of the title - Biblical language just confuses me. I think this play raises interesting ideas, was probably very revolutionary at the time it was published.... but I'm just too old and liberal and jaded to be reading it in 2009. Two things that really bothered me:<br /><br />1) What's up with Rachel? She's such a boring little ingenue, hammering us over the head with how torn she is between doctrine and instinct, and you just want to slap her in the face and tell her to grow a pair. The preacher's daughter is torn between what's she been taught all her life, and her new experiences that contradict those teachings.... have we not seen this a million times? Was that not already a kind of stock character at that point in history? The two lawyers are actually really well-developed characters, as is most of the supporting cast, but Rachel, and Bert too, are just like these boring placeholders. If Bert's such a brave and smart guy, then why does he come off like such a milquetoast?<br /><br />2) Did the William Jennings Bryan character really have to keel over and die right after the trial? Wikipedia says he died in his sleep a few days later. I'm impressed at how they were able to make him so sympathetic, but the death was melodramatic.<br /><br />There's a lot of great stuff in this play, but it could use an update - maybe it could be more subtle on those points. I'm sure I would have loved it in high school, though.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17866066839044761824" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_564582984" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Alexis Kloehn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12743401-alexis-kloehn"><img alt="Alexis Kloehn" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/564582984?book_show_action=true">Mar 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alexis Kloehn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alexis Kloehn" href="/user/show/12743401-alexis-kloehn">Alexis Kloehn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer564582984" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7237731934629083363">“Inherit the Wind” is one of the few plays we have read as a class that I actually enjoyed. Most of the time I don’t even read the plays or books we’re reading in class. They are always boring and old plays or books about stuff I don’t understand or care to understand. This was not one of them. The main reasons I liked this play is that it was about a real life experience that actually happened. Another reason I liked it was because of the themes and messages it gave. One of the most important t</span>
  <span id="freeText7237731934629083363" style="display:none">“Inherit the Wind” is one of the few plays we have read as a class that I actually enjoyed. Most of the time I don’t even read the plays or books we’re reading in class. They are always boring and old plays or books about stuff I don’t understand or care to understand. This was not one of them. The main reasons I liked this play is that it was about a real life experience that actually happened. Another reason I liked it was because of the themes and messages it gave. One of the most important things to me is the fact that I am able to make my own decisions on basically everything. I am not forced to do anything too major that I don’t want. I am able to make my own decisions when it comes to deciding what I am doing with my life. My parents and other people that are close to me influence me to do what they believe is right. I always have the final decision on it and that is important. This play also is a good way to show that if you believe in a topic or don’t believe in it you should stand up and say something about it. I recommend this play to high school students because it is a great way to show people that they should stand up for what they believe in and take pride in it. This would actually be a good book for anyone to read and I would recommend it because I enjoyed it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7237731934629083363" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_559105594" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/559105594" />
    <a title="Logan Erdmann" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12743397-logan-erdmann"><img alt="Logan Erdmann" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer559105594" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6641486017161140817">I would say that this play is okay and I would recommend it to older readers who are interested in the law. "Inherit the Wind" was based off The John Scopes trial in 1925. The trial was about teaching evolution in the classroom and that a teacher had broken the law by teaching it. This book was really about creation verse revolution and that the two ideas can co-exist. The play had many symbols in the plot that resembled a thought or idea. For example, Henry Drummond wore purple suspenders that </span>
  <span id="freeText6641486017161140817" style="display:none">I would say that this play is okay and I would recommend it to older readers who are interested in the law. "Inherit the Wind" was based off The John Scopes trial in 1925. The trial was about teaching evolution in the classroom and that a teacher had broken the law by teaching it. This book was really about creation verse revolution and that the two ideas can co-exist. The play had many symbols in the plot that resembled a thought or idea. For example, Henry Drummond wore purple suspenders that Brady picked on him for but, they were from Matthew Brady's hometown.I thought this was a very well put together play that allowed me to have my own opinion on the subject.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6641486017161140817" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1540371949" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9867170953466670576">This play is kinda mean-spirited and condescending, one-sided in its presentation of its villains (as in an Ayn Rand novel). The stage directions are impositions, authoritarian and smug. <br/><br/>But, then again—then again—this text has revealed to me two of my basic operating parameters in:<br/><br/><i>Hello Devil. Welcome to Hell.</i> (which is kinda how it is to be a marxist-in-cappyland);<br/><br/>and—how not?—<br/><br/><blockquote>Drummond: What do you think?<br/>Brady: I do not think about things that … I do not think about!<br/>Drummond: Do you ever think</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText9867170953466670576" style="display:none">This play is kinda mean-spirited and condescending, one-sided in its presentation of its villains (as in an Ayn Rand novel). The stage directions are impositions, authoritarian and smug. <br /><br />But, then again—then again—this text has revealed to me two of my basic operating parameters in:<br /><br /><i>Hello Devil. Welcome to Hell.</i> (which is kinda how it is to be a marxist-in-cappyland);<br /><br />and—how not?—<br /><br /><blockquote>Drummond: What do you think?<br />Brady: I do not think about things that … I do not think about!<br />Drummond: Do you ever think about things that you do think about?</blockquote> I kinda walk around all day thinking that last line in response to all the lumpenized antisocial nihilism.<br /><br />As an attorney, am completely sympathizing with Drummond’s lament that “someday I’m going to get me an easy case.”<br /><br />Text is equally concerned with the progress of science v. religion under the law as it is with mass communication of the debate as a public <i>agon</i> (journalist and radio guy are characters given some significant attention).<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9867170953466670576" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer403893966" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer13025032009471850903">What a thrilling, dramatic play! I absolutely loved it; it's the kind of play you can see unfold in your head with startling detail, like you're sitting at the foot of the stage watching the whole thing up close. I especially loved the courtroom scenes and the way it ended, with the two books in the briefcase, sitting side-by-side like it's natural for them to be together - God and Darwin. I would give my left leg to actually see Inherit The Wind live and on stage; I imagine it would take quite </span>
  <span id="freeText13025032009471850903" style="display:none">What a thrilling, dramatic play! I absolutely loved it; it's the kind of play you can see unfold in your head with startling detail, like you're sitting at the foot of the stage watching the whole thing up close. I especially loved the courtroom scenes and the way it ended, with the two books in the briefcase, sitting side-by-side like it's natural for them to be together - God and Darwin. I would give my left leg to actually see Inherit The Wind live and on stage; I imagine it would take quite a dynamic cast to bring this play to life properly.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer396304475661384560">“Inherit the Wind” is about a teacher, Bertram Cates, who didn’t follow the Butler Act that stated evolution couldn’t be taught in a public school. Drummond defends Cates against the “top dog” Brady in the trial. The story takes place in Hillsboro, TN around the year 1925.   <span id="freeText396304475661384560" style="display:none">“Inherit the Wind” is about a teacher, Bertram Cates, who didn’t follow the Butler Act that stated evolution couldn’t be taught in a public school. Drummond defends Cates against the “top dog” Brady in the trial. The story takes place in Hillsboro, TN around the year 1925. Cates is fighting for his freedom to teach his students to think for themselves instead of just accepting one idea. I would recommend this drama to high school students, because they would understand it better and the vocabulary is more difficult. If you like learning about evolution, you would like this play.</span>

<div id="review_2212054799" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2212054799" />
    <a title="Austin Krause" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/46817013-austin-krause"><img alt="Austin Krause" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1461108097p2/46817013.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2212054799?book_show_action=true">Dec 12, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Austin Krause" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Austin Krause" href="/user/show/46817013-austin-krause">Austin Krause</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2212054799" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9328425011616683820">Personal Response:<br/>I really enjoyed this drama. My enjoyment was likely helped by the style of reading we did to complete the play. By reading a specific part, I was more involved in the storyline and found more fun in it than I would have by reading it on my own. I also liked the topic of the play because it is still an issue in today’s world.<br/><br/>Plot:<br/>The opening scene of the drama showed the division between Darwinism and Creationism with Howard and Melinda. The setting then moved to the courthouse</span>
  <span id="freeText9328425011616683820" style="display:none">Personal Response:<br />I really enjoyed this drama. My enjoyment was likely helped by the style of reading we did to complete the play. By reading a specific part, I was more involved in the storyline and found more fun in it than I would have by reading it on my own. I also liked the topic of the play because it is still an issue in today’s world.<br /><br />Plot:<br />The opening scene of the drama showed the division between Darwinism and Creationism with Howard and Melinda. The setting then moved to the courthouse where Rachel Brown was seeing her boyfriend, Bertram Cates. Once again, the setting changed to the town square, where many of the residents of Hillsboro Tennessee were preparing a large welcoming ceremony. Banners were put up, food prepared, and people sang Christian hymns. Only now did Jerome Lawrence and Robert E. Lee reveal who the welcome ceremony was for. Matthew Harrison Brady, a highly decorated Bible speaker, was coming to Hillsboro to prosecute the trial of Bertram Cates. Upon his arrival, there was music and song, food, drink, and cheer as if Brady was God showing up at a wedding ceremony. Soon, the cheer ended and most of the townspeople retired to their homes for the night. Melinda did not at that time, and she was the one that introduced Henry Drummond to the plot, but not in a nice way like Brady. Brady was welcomed like God, but Drummond was portrayed as the Devil. This difference followed Brady and Drummond into the courtroom and remained in place for the majority of the trial. Brady got a lot of good hints from his witnesses, but Drummond took it easy and asked simple questions that made witnesses think between creationism and evolution. Drummond soon found out that Brady, although very knowledgeable in the Bible, had very little knowledge of Darwin’s theory. During his time to call witnesses, Drummond’s plans went bad and the only person he could call that he could use to his advantage was Brady. Brady, because of his hubris, gladly accepted the challenge, but was completely blown away by Drummond’s questioning. Drummond managed to come up with three occasions mentioned in the Bible about creation that could also be explained by evolution and revealed to the audience that Brady was not all he was cut out to be. After questioning, the jury still ruled Bertram Cates guilty of violating the Butler Act and was fined a measly $100 fine, but Drummond appealed to the Supreme Court and E.K. Hornbeck paid Cates’ bond to remove Cates from jail for the time before the appeal. Brady was left in shock that Drummond made such a fool of him, and he no longer had any of the followers he had when he came to Hillsboro. All the people were now on Drummond’s side. Then, during a last attempt at a victory speech, Brady fainted and was carried off to the doctor’s house. Only hours later, the judge returned to the courthouse bearing news that Brady had died of a heart attack. With that, Drummond closed up shop and got ready to leave Hillsboro in preparation for the appeal trial. Cates and Rachel also got ready to leave Hillsboro to live elsewhere together. The closing image summed up the entire play in a nutshell. Drummond stood on the courtroom stairs and held out the Bible and a copy of Darwin’s <i>Origin of Species</i> at the same level showing just how equal the two books had become.<br /><br />Characterization:<br />In the drama, Matthew Harrison Brady was one of the most important roles in the play. Without his presence at the court, the case of Cates vs. the State of Tennessee probably would not have drawn so much attention to itself. Brady’s arrival to Hillsboro was a huge deal for the people because Brady was their lives. He was confident and everyone loved him and his teachings. That soon changed for Brady, during the course of the trial, he was beaten and battered very violently by Henry Drummond’s defense. At the end of the trial, Brady was metaphorically dead. He was completely defeated, despite his win, and had close to zero support remaining on his side. It may have been this stress that helped bring about Brady’s death just hours after the trial.<br /><br />Effects of Setting on Plot:<br />The place that this play was set in was essential to the result of the trial. Hillsboro was a hugely religious town in Tennessee. The state of Tennessee had passed a law that made it illegal to teach evolution in a public school. The jury in the trial was biased toward creationism, regardless of how hard Drummond tried to get people on his side of the deal, which ultimately made the result unfair. This situation could have happened anywhere in the United States at the time, but southern states had closer ties with religion and the Bible than northern states did. The time period was not extremely important in regards to where the United States was physically, but mentally, at the time, people were starting to open their minds more to new ideas. All over the world, people opened themselves up to new ideas such as evolution, and this caused a lot of controversy in governments and schools. 1920s governments at the time, however, did not like this change in thought from people and passed the Butler Act making it illegal to teach evolution in Tennessee public schools.<br /><br />Recommendation:<br />I would recommend this drama to really anyone. There is no hint of gender specification for the reading audience. In general, age is no big factor for a reader, the word choice is pretty basic. The only reason for anyone to not read this book is for the same reason the book was written, that being censorship.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9328425011616683820" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2251970299" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2251970299" />
    <a title="Natalie Schneider" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/46645575-natalie-schneider"><img alt="Natalie Schneider" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2251970299?book_show_action=true">Jan 10, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Natalie Schneider" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Natalie Schneider" href="/user/show/46645575-natalie-schneider">Natalie Schneider</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2251970299" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer289346730853385464">Personal Response: After reading <i>Inherit the Wind </i>, I thought it was a great play. Even though it was written quite a few years ago, I still thought there were many great lessons that still apply today. I really liked the way the authors wrote this play. They were detailed and had some humor with every scene that made it have a good balance. <br/>Plot: Overall this drama was based on an actual case called the Scopes Trial. <i>Inherit the Wind </i> started with two young kids named Howard and Melinda standin</span>
  <span id="freeText289346730853385464" style="display:none">Personal Response: After reading <i>Inherit the Wind </i>, I thought it was a great play. Even though it was written quite a few years ago, I still thought there were many great lessons that still apply today. I really liked the way the authors wrote this play. They were detailed and had some humor with every scene that made it have a good balance. <br />Plot: Overall this drama was based on an actual case called the Scopes Trial. <i>Inherit the Wind </i> started with two young kids named Howard and Melinda standing in the courtyard. Howard accused Melinda of being a worm. Then Rachel entered and asked a bailiff to see Bert Cates in jail, but she was hiding this visit from her father who was the reverend. Rachel tried to get Cates to confess to his wrongdoing because Matthew Harrison Brady was going to prosecute him. Cates thought he did nothing wrong teaching Darwin’s Theory of Evolution though. <br />Meanwhile, the town of Hillsboro was preparing for Brady’s arrival. The whole town was very excited for him to come. When Brady and his wife arrived, the town had a potluck for him. At the potluck, E.K. Hornbeck, a newspaper writer, told Brady he was going against Henry Drummond in court. No one in the town liked Drummond. When Drummond arrived, no one cheered or rejoiced in his arrival. Everyone thought he was the devil actually. <br />The next day, both sides chose their jurors. Then the judge called for a recess due to an argument that arose in the courtroom. Then both sides had their witnesses on the stand and questioned them. Throughout the whole questioning, Brady and Drummond were arguing and constantly tried to prove they were right. Brady thought that there was no such thing as Darwin’s Theory, and thought that it was wrong to teach the theory to students. Drummond thought that people should be able to have the freedom to learn both sides and decide which side they believed. <br />The next day both sides waited for the verdict. He was found guilty and received a fine of one hundred dollars. When the court was adjourned, Brady was trying to give a speech but no one listened to him. He was so upset and frustrated that he collapsed. He had a heart attack and died. Rachel entered the room with Cates and Drummond. She announced that she read Darwin and realized she should not be afraid to think for herself. Then Rachel and Cates left on a train together. Drummond was left alone in the courtroom and had both the bible and a copy of Darwin. The playwright ended with Drummond putting both books in his briefcase. <br />Characterization: One of the main characters was Matthew Harrison Brady. He was the prosecuting attorney. He had a lot of pride and loved all the attention that the people of Hillsboro gave him. Also, he was very outgoing. Throughout the play, he became too arrogant and had too much pride. His personality was all shine and no substance. On the outside he seemed very smart and a well rounded individual. In reality though, Brady had no knowledge of Drummond’s side of the case and not a lot of knowledge of his own side too. Eventually, he had a downfall because of having too much pride at the end of the play. <br />Another main character was Henry Drummond. He was the defense attorney. He was basically the complete opposite of Brady. The town hated Drummond unlike Brady. He did not receive a warm welcoming when he arrived in Hillsboro. He was not that outgoing. He kept to himself out times and did not seek the attention. He was quite humble actually. Drummond was also very smart. He knew both sides of the case unlike Brady. Drummond had pride in his work but not too much that he had a downfall. <br /> Setting: <i>Inherit the Wind </i> took place in the summer at Hillsboro, Tennessee in the year 1925. The time of the year was important to the plot line because there was some references of the heat in the playwright. The place where it took place was also important to the plot line. This was important because it was a small, buckle belt town in America that had the whole nation’s attention due to the court trial. The year was important because that was when the actual Scopes Trial took place against the Butler Act. If the case did not happen, then the Butler Act would not have been questioned. Also another major setting was in the courthouse or near it. Majority of the plot took place in the courtroom or in the courtyard. This was important because the whole plot was based on the court case dealing with the Butler Act. <br />Recommendation: I would recommend this drama to any gender who was in high school. The read was not too hard, but did take some skill to understand some of the literary terms like the analogies or metaphors. I thought this drama was gender neutral too. <br /></span>

<div id="review_2662788333" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2662788333" />
    <a title="Lily Mueller" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/71164834-lily-mueller"><img alt="Lily Mueller" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2662788333?book_show_action=true">Jan 07, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lily Mueller" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lily Mueller" href="/user/show/71164834-lily-mueller">Lily Mueller</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2662788333&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2662788333" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7270763845665645836">Personal Response: I was very unexcited for this <i>Inherit the Wind</i>. I usually don’t go for historical based books. <i>Inherit the Wind</i> completely surprised me. Though it was based on an event that happened a while ago, I could relate it to more modern day issues that are going on. I enjoyed how the authors took the opportunity to make a complicated and a long court case into an interesting and suspenseful read. The book was well written and thought out. I ended up really enjoying reading <i>Inherit the</i></span>
  <span id="freeText7270763845665645836" style="display:none">Personal Response: I was very unexcited for this <i>Inherit the Wind</i>. I usually don’t go for historical based books. <i>Inherit the Wind</i> completely surprised me. Though it was based on an event that happened a while ago, I could relate it to more modern day issues that are going on. I enjoyed how the authors took the opportunity to make a complicated and a long court case into an interesting and suspenseful read. The book was well written and thought out. I ended up really enjoying reading <i>Inherit the Wind</i> .<br /><br />Plot Summary: <i>Inherit the Wind</i> is based on the Scopes Trial of 1925. Bertram Cates, who represented John Scopes, is put in jail for violating the Butler Act and teaching evolution in his classroom. A powerful prosecuting attorney by the name of Matthew Harrison Brady, who represented William Jennings Bryan, is put on the trial. He is welcomed with open arms into the town and even given the title Colonel. On the other hand, Henry Drummond, who represented Clarence Darrow, is the defense attorney. The townspeople are unhappy with his presence and even a bit frightened by him. The trial begins and right away it seems as if Brady has the case in the beg. Brady is very confident that he will win the case. Halfway through the case, all of Drummond’s defendants are rejected by the prosecuting and the judge. Drummond has no choice but to put Brady on the stand as his last hope. By doing this, he intends to get into Brady’s head and it works. Towards the end of the session, the courtroom is uneven. People laugh at Brady, but they don’t necessarily praise Drummond either. The next day the jury decides the verdict. Cates is found guilty of violating the Butler Act, but will only be given a fine of $100 and no jail time. Brady thinks this is an outrage and demands the case not to be closed. The court is adjourned, but Brady is trying to give a speech that no one will listen to. The radio service even cuts him off. Brady is no longer found important to the townspeople, which causes him a great amount of stress. The stress then causes his “belly to bust,” and soon after he dies of his injuries. Rachel, Cates’ friend and possible love interest, runs into the courtroom and tells Cates that she's leaving her father and running away and tells Cates to come with her. <br /><br />Characterization: Rachel is one character in this play that really stands out to me. She is well over the adult age, but still feels controlled by her father. She feels that because her father is the Reverend of the town; she has to abide by his rules and not share her views with him. In <i>Inherit the Wind</i> readers can see that Rachel finds herself and rises above her father. Rachel is sweet, soft and caring. From the start of the play, Rachel wants to be able to tell her father she doesn’t agree with him. When her father condemns her, and Cates to hell she decides enough is enough. She rises above him and knows she can decide her life for herself. Readers can see her strength, shine through finally. Matthew Harrison Brady also goes through a change. When he arrives in Hillsboro, Brady thinks he is a very important person. As the trial winds down, readers see that he isn’t all that he’s cracked up to be. Brady loses his fame and importance and feels what it’s like to come in second instead of first. <br /><br />Setting: <i>Inherit the Wind</i> takes place in a fictional town called Hillsboro, Tennessee. In real life, this play took place in Dayton, Tennessee. The town is significant because Hillsboro, Tennessee is considered to be very conservative and religious. The story takes place in the summer of 1925. The season is important to the storyline because there are a few references where they compare the temperature to the tension in the courtroom. <br /><br />Recommendation: I would recommend this play to anyone in high school mainly because there are deeper meanings to a lot of the subject matter. Younger readers might not fully grasp the book. This book is appropriate for both females and males, as it does have universal subject matter.</span>

<div id="review_3127371625" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3127371625" />
    <a title="Hannah Schuelke" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/71454877-hannah-schuelke"><img alt="Hannah Schuelke" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3127371625?book_show_action=true">Jan 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hannah Schuelke" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hannah Schuelke" href="/user/show/71454877-hannah-schuelke">Hannah Schuelke</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer16739833620500223944"><br/><br/>Personal Response: <br/> I think<i>Inherit the Wind</i> by Jerome Lawrence and Robert E. Lee is a great book for English class. I believe this because it is a very powerful drama written after a true event. It gives readers their own perspective and opinion on things that happen in the book. The book displays a lot of symbolism and themes throughout the story. It is fun to read then discuss what my classmates and I think something means from the book. <br/><br/><br/>Plot Summary:<br/> <i>Inherit the Wind</i> is based on the real Scop</span>
  <span id="freeText16739833620500223944" style="display:none"><br /><br />Personal Response: <br /> I think<i>Inherit the Wind</i> by Jerome Lawrence and Robert E. Lee is a great book for English class. I believe this because it is a very powerful drama written after a true event. It gives readers their own perspective and opinion on things that happen in the book. The book displays a lot of symbolism and themes throughout the story. It is fun to read then discuss what my classmates and I think something means from the book. <br /><br /><br />Plot Summary:<br /> <i>Inherit the Wind</i> is based on the real Scopes Monkey Trial, which takes place in 1926. The trial is about Bertram Cates breaking the Butler Act and teaching his public students about Darwin´s theory of evolution. This is against the law at this time, because it denies creation.<br />The town is very religious and takes Cates’ crime very seriously. They have a huge trial about it, even though they already know what side they support. The townspeople bring in Matthew Brady to be their prosecuting attorney and defend their law. The people treat Brady like a king, because they decorate and prepare food for his arrival. While the defense attorney, Henry Drummond, is treated the exact opposite. The people do not like him or treat him fairly. The court case is full of heat because the people obviously favor Brady and do not care about Drummond´s points until Drummond calls Brady to the stand and exposes him. The people begin to turn on Brady and the trial is now fair. The jury still finds Cates guilty, but he only faces a small fine. <br /><br /><br />Characterization: <br /> Henry Drummond is a famous lawyer who believes people have the right to think. The <i>Baltimore Herald</i> sends Drummond to this trial because he takes a lot of underdog cases. Drummond is seen as an agnostic but leaves the town as a hero. The people do not like him right away; however, Drummond exposes Brady and tells them about their right to think, the people begin to thank him and turn on Brady. This case makes Drummond realize that both books have equal values. <br />	<br /> Matthew Brady is a national political figure who comes to Hillsboro as the prosecuting attorney in this case. Brady has a strong faith in the Bible and God the Father. The people love Brady and prepare the whole town for his coming. The jury is on Brady´s side until he is exposed. Brady does not know as much about the <i>Bible</i> as he claims. The people begin to turn on Brady, which actually makes this case fair. <br /><br /> Bertram Cates is the victim of the story, and he is on trial. He does not seem like the type of person to be thrown in jail, but he breaks the law. He teaches evolution to his class, which breaks the law of denying God the Father. He wants the students to know both sides and have the freedom to choose what they believe. <br /><br />Setting:<br /> The setting of this book takes place in summer, not too long ago in Hillsboro, Tennessee. The book is a play about the real-life Scopes trial in July 1925 in Dayton, Tennessee. The setting and weather do affect the trial and even cause some irony. The courtroom is heated and the trial didn´t even start yet. The book takes place before cellphones and the internet, which affects their communication. This trial spread throughout the country because it appears in newspapers and is a worldwide case. The trial affects all of the characters in different ways, but this case has made an impact on each of their lives. <br /><br />Recommendation: <br /> At first, I thought I would not like reading this book, but I enjoy reading about this case and the effects it has on the characters. I think this is a good book to read in class because it is important to discuss things that happen in the book and make sure readers understand them. I would recommend this book to high schoolers because there is a lot of background information the readers need to understand before they read this book. </span>

    <a title="Austin Walters" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59370697-austin-walters"><img alt="Austin Walters" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1137619126166258528">Personal Response:<br/>I think Jerome Lawrence and Robert E. Lee do a very good job of taking a real-life event and turning it into a fun and yet true drama: <i>Inherit The Wind</i>. I think the name of this book, <i>Inherit The Wind</i>, really is true and shows how important it is to not cause trouble for others. One way this is proven is how Brady gets a lot of trouble and later dies of a bust belly. The authors do an amazing job of putting the real facts in this book in a creative way. The authors, in my opini</span>
  <span id="freeText1137619126166258528" style="display:none">Personal Response:<br />I think Jerome Lawrence and Robert E. Lee do a very good job of taking a real-life event and turning it into a fun and yet true drama: <i>Inherit The Wind</i>. I think the name of this book, <i>Inherit The Wind</i>, really is true and shows how important it is to not cause trouble for others. One way this is proven is how Brady gets a lot of trouble and later dies of a bust belly. The authors do an amazing job of putting the real facts in this book in a creative way. The authors, in my opinion, have made a book that is captivating to many audiences. My favorite part of the book is the fact that the characters change to see the good in Drummond and Brady's hubris. I like how this book is very fast paced with a lot going on at any time.<br /><br />Plot:<br />This book is about the Scopes Trial in 1925. Ready to tell his class the truth, Cates reads from a board-approved textbook about evolution. After doing so, cates is charged and “convicted” of violating the Butler Act. The town quickly judges Cates as a horrible person and a criminal; however, everyone soon sees after Drummond shows them that Cates is not a bad person. He does this by showing them that evolution is not right or wrong. With everyone learning quickly that Brady is not so perfect and knowledgeable about the <i>Bible</i>, he is abandoned as people start to see the reality of the situation rather than the appearance that Brady tries to show. Drummond shows how the <i>Bible</i> can be interpreted in more than one way without proving the <i>Bible</i> to be false. He also shows the importance of being able to think for oneself and how it is okay to have an opinion if it is an informed one.<br /><br />Characterization:<br />The book is told from the first person point of view. The main characters are: Rachel, Brady, Drummond, and Cates. Their characters experience many hard decisions that most can relate to such as deciding to conform to the group or stand alone, even when everyone is watching. Their decisions later impact their entire lives and their beliefs. Their characters go through several hardships. They handle the pressure as best as they can and finds ways to cope with the harshness of his life. Rachel and Cates are protagonists because they are helping solve the problem of conformity. Brady and Reverend Brown are antagonists because they only judge and point fingers rather than being open to new ideas.<br /><br />Setting:<br /><i>Inherit The Wind</i> takes place in a small town, Hillsboro, Tennessee, not too long ago. The setting is important to the storyline because Tennessee is the “Buckle on the <i>Bible</i> Belt.” The setting shows the judgments that are premade against southerners as being conservative. There are references to bigger cities because they are looking at the Butler Act as a bad law that is unconstitutional. Brady never talks about the place he lives as Drummond shows with the purple suspenders. The time period is in the 1920s; however, it is not significant because the issues discussed are concepts relatable to any time period. These problems are always there one way or another.<br /><br />Recommendation: <br />I would recommend <i>Inherit The Wind</i> to eighth grade and beyond who are not close-minded because some of the subjects require someone to think beyond his own belief. Both males and females will enjoy this book because the main characters are male and female. It also has universal subject matter and relatable characters.</span>

<div id="review_1940035583" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

<span id="freeTextContainer10865007597028855392">Personal Response<br/>I read the book <i> Inherit The Wind </i> by Jerome Lawrence and Robert E. Lee. This wasn’t my favorite book. I wasn’t interested in the topics talked about in the book. I felt like I couldn’t connect with the characters. <br/><br/>Plot<br/>Bertram Cates was arrested for teaching evolution in Hillsboro, Tennessee while the Butler Act was in place. He lived in a town that was very religious. His girlfriend, Rachel Brown, tried to persuade him to admit he was guilty and call the whole thing off. Cates </span>
  <span id="freeText10865007597028855392" style="display:none">Personal Response<br />I read the book <i> Inherit The Wind </i> by Jerome Lawrence and Robert E. Lee. This wasn’t my favorite book. I wasn’t interested in the topics talked about in the book. I felt like I couldn’t connect with the characters. <br /><br />Plot<br />Bertram Cates was arrested for teaching evolution in Hillsboro, Tennessee while the Butler Act was in place. He lived in a town that was very religious. His girlfriend, Rachel Brown, tried to persuade him to admit he was guilty and call the whole thing off. Cates refused because he thought the children should have both theories be taught to them. Matthew Harrison Brady became the prosecuting attorney. The whole town worshipped Brady since he was also religious. A few ladies put together a lunch for Brady. While at the lunch, Brady comforted Rachel, and received information about Cates. E. K. Hornbeck, a writer for the <i> Baltimore Harold </i>, traveled to Hillsboro. The town found out that Henry Drummond, an attorney who took underdog cases, was going to defend Cates. When Drummond arrived, Melinda ran up to him and called him the devil. When the trial started, Brady felt confident because he had the whole town on his side. Reverend Brown held a prayer circle. At the meeting he condemned Bertram Cates and his own daughter. When the witnesses are called at the trial, Brady twisted Rachel’s words and made Cates look even worse. Drummond brought scientists who knew about the theory of evolution. The judge dismissed all of Drummond’s witnesses because they had nothing to do with the trial. Since he had no witnesses, Drummond called Brady to the stand. Brady was so full of confidence he agreed. Drummond revealed that Brady did not know anything about the theory of evolution. The townspeople started to agree with Drummond. Cates was found guilty, and fined $100. Brady thought he should have had a larger punishment. Wanting to be the center of attention, Brady started saying a speech. Towards the end however, Brady collapsed and was removed from the courtroom. Brady later died from a busted belly. Rachel admitted she tried to read Darwin’s <i> On Origin Of Species</i>. Rachel and Cates got on a train and left Hillsboro. Drummond left the courtroom while balancing the Bible in one hand, and Darwin’s <i> On Origin Of Species</i> in the other. <br /><br />Characterization<br />Matthew Harrison Brady was liked by the whole town when he first showed up. He did not like change, and wanted everyone to believe the same thing. He was the prosecuting attorney who only accepted cases he knew he would win. Mr. Brady was a very selfish man. He also ran for presidency three times. <br />Henry Drummond was a defense attorney who helped the underdogs. He knew Bertram Cates was going to lose, but he took his case anyway. Mr. Drummond was a nonconformist. He was okay with leaving everyone behind and going his own way. <br /><br />Setting<br />This book took place in Hillsboro, Tennessee. The author wrote that it took place not too long ago. The setting was important to the story because the Butler Act was a law at this time in the south. <br /><br />Recommendation <br />I would recommend this book to boys and girls in high school. This book contained a more mature plot. This book did have some swearing in it. Both boys and girls would like it because they could relate to the idea of standing up for what they believe in.</span>
