<span id="freeTextContainer17807147718036935134">Personal Response:<br/>I thought <i> Inherit The Wind </i> wasn’t the greatest drama that I have ever read but still was decent. The plot bounced around a lot, such as in scene three the plot jumped from Cates being arraigned in scene two to the Prosecution calling its last witness. I enjoyed the part when Drummond was questioning Brady on the stand as Drummond had some pretty good wisecracks for Brady.<br/><br/>Plot Summary:<br/><i> Inherit The Wind </i> starts out by foreshadowing the battle that is to come by having two kids </span>
  <span id="freeText17807147718036935134" style="display:none">Personal Response:<br />I thought <i> Inherit The Wind </i> wasn’t the greatest drama that I have ever read but still was decent. The plot bounced around a lot, such as in scene three the plot jumped from Cates being arraigned in scene two to the Prosecution calling its last witness. I enjoyed the part when Drummond was questioning Brady on the stand as Drummond had some pretty good wisecracks for Brady.<br /><br />Plot Summary:<br /><i> Inherit The Wind </i> starts out by foreshadowing the battle that is to come by having two kids having an argument where Howard represents evolution and Melinda represents creationism. After that, a woman named Rachel Brown is at the courthouse and going to see her boyfriend who is in jail. Her boyfriend, Bertram Cates, is in jail for teaching evolution to his high school biology class. The setting then changes to people getting ready for a festival of some sort. They are getting the town ready for when Matthew Harrison Brady arrives. They have a feast ready, have a band playing music, and a big banner that is hung up. Brady is also given the honorable title of Colonel. It is revealed that he is going to prosecute Cates’ trial. A newspaper reporter named Hornbeck tells the townspeople during the feast that Henry Drummond was coming to the town for the defense. When Drummond arrives, he receives the exact opposite that Brady did. Melinda is the only one who is present when he arrives, and she calls him the devil. The setting then jumps to Cates’ arraignment. Drummond objects to Brady’s honorable name, and he receives the same title. The plot once again changes rapidly, this time to the trial. When Brady is questioning Howard, he doesn’t allow Howard to think for himself. When Drummond questions him, Drummond allows Howard to answer the way he wants to. Brady then calls Rachel to the stand. Brady’s constant drilling causes Rachel to breakdown, and Drummond doesn’t have the chance to cross examine her. When Drummond calls his witnesses to the stand, the court rules that all of his witnesses have no relevance to the case. Drummond calls Brady to the stand as an expert on the bible. Brady defiantly takes the stand, even though he doesn’t have to. Drummond questioning Brady leads to Brady’s downfall. When the testimony is over, the jury finds Cates guilty. The judge sentences Cates to a one hundred dollar fine. While Brady is giving a speech at the end, he collapses, and dies. The drama ends with Cates and Rachel leaving the town on a train.<br /><br />Characterization:<br />In this drama, the most important characters are Matthew Harrison Brady and Henry Drummond. Brady is very popular with religious people and was a presidential candidate. In the beginning he is confident that he will win the case. In an act of hubris, he causes his own downfall when he allows Drummond to call him to the witness stand. Henry Drummond is the exact opposite of Brady. Drummond is agnostic and believes people should be able to think on their own.<br /><br />Setting:<br />The importance of the setting on the plot is that if the play did not take place in Tennessee, Cates would not have been jailed for teaching evolution. The drama takes place in 1925, which makes the teaching of evolution illegal in Tennessee.<br /><br />Recommendation:<br />I would recommend this drama to just about everybody. The reading level is not very high, and both males and females could enjoy the drama. Drummond does have some foul language at times, but nothing too horrible.</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer8773470201338247569">Personal Response:<br/><i>Inherit The Wind</i> was an excellent drama about the Scopes Trial. In 1925, John Scopes was brought to trial in Dayton, Tennessee. He was accused of violating the Butler Act. The law stated that evolution could not be taught in a public school, and he was found guilty. I appreciated the underdog feel the book gave off. After Matthew Brady’s <br/>joyous reception, Henry Drummond arrived only to be greeted by a girl who called him the devil. Drummond was the defense attorney in the book </span>
  <span id="freeText8773470201338247569" style="display:none">Personal Response:<br /><i>Inherit The Wind</i> was an excellent drama about the Scopes Trial. In 1925, John Scopes was brought to trial in Dayton, Tennessee. He was accused of violating the Butler Act. The law stated that evolution could not be taught in a public school, and he was found guilty. I appreciated the underdog feel the book gave off. After Matthew Brady’s <br />joyous reception, Henry Drummond arrived only to be greeted by a girl who called him the devil. Drummond was the defense attorney in the book while Matthew Harrison Brady was the prosecuting attorney in the book.<br /><br />Plot:<br />The drama had a controversial plot. Bertram Cates, a fictional character of John Scopes, was put on trial for teaching evolution. He violated the Butler Act which was against the law. At first, the case seemed clear-cut after Matthew Harrison Brady, a fictional character of William Jennings Bryan, was brought in as the prosecutor; however, the <i>Baltimore Herald</i> hired Henry Drummond as the defense attorney and brought in a reporter: E.K. Hornbeck. Matthew Brady was well loved by the religious community, but Henry Drummond was known to take underdog cases and win. Henry Drummond leveled the playing field at the start of the case. At one point, Drummond’s defense crumbled; however, Brady was too arrogant and took to the stand when he should not have. This action revived Drummond’s defense and affected the verdict. There were many instances of foreshadowing throughout the book. After Reverend Brown, Rachel’s Father, gave a radical speech, Matthew Harrison Brady said, “He that troubleth his own house, shall inherit the wind.” He said this because if someone caused trouble, trouble happened to them.<br /><br />Characterization:<br />Rachel, Bertram Cate’s girlfriend, was afraid of her father at the start of the book. She believed anything her father believed. Towards the end of the book, Rachel was more independent and began to think for herself. More importantly, she was less afraid of her father. <br /><br />Matthew Harrison Brady was an interesting character. At first, he was very arrogant and prideful of himself. When Drummond revealed how shallow Brady was, the community of Hillsboro gave Drummond more attention. Brady’s pride was broken and it led to his demise. <br /><br /><br /><br />Impacts of Setting:<br />The book took place in Hillsboro, Tennessee in 1925. The first ever radio announcement for a trial verdict was announced in the book. Older transportation and clothing were mentioned in the book. The setting played an important role in the outcome of the book. Hillsboro was in the middle of the bible belt. As a result, the community was very religious. Henry Drummond and Bertram Cates, the defendant, were already at a disadvantage, because they had to convince a religious community to accept evolution.<br /><br />Recommendation:<br />I recommend this book to females ages 14-18. Anyone younger than 14 would not understand some of the themes or symbols introduced in the book. People older than 18 would think this book is below their reading abilities. Females would like this book more, because the book is dynamic and makes the reader think.<br /></span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15724753791608367221"><br/><br/>PERSONAL RESPONSE:<br/><i>Inherit the Wind</i> was the first play that I truly enjoyed. The main reason I liked this play was that it was about a real-life experience. Therefore, it presented the play from a unique viewpoint. Also, I enjoyed reading the themes and messages presented. For instance, the theme of conformity vs nonconformity caused me to question my everyday customs. This is because one unconsciously follows along others social standards in order to avoid judgment or to feel accepted.<br/><br/>PLOT:<br/>The s</span>
  <span id="freeText15724753791608367221" style="display:none"><br /><br />PERSONAL RESPONSE:<br /><i>Inherit the Wind</i> was the first play that I truly enjoyed. The main reason I liked this play was that it was about a real-life experience. Therefore, it presented the play from a unique viewpoint. Also, I enjoyed reading the themes and messages presented. For instance, the theme of conformity vs nonconformity caused me to question my everyday customs. This is because one unconsciously follows along others social standards in order to avoid judgment or to feel accepted.<br /><br />PLOT:<br />The small town of Hillsboro was a quiet town that did not have notable occurrences. Although, it became fully recognized by both the nation and media after Bert Cates decided to teach evolution to his class. The townspeople perceived his teachings as a menace as it contradicted the Butler Act. The situation escalated to the point where the case was brought into a whole new level as two of the nation's leading lawyers went head to head. Matthew Harrison Brady was the prosecuting attorney and Henry Drummond was the defense attorney. Bertram Cate’s trial became the essence of contradiction about religious fundamentalism versus the freedom of individual thought.<br /><br />CHARACTERIZATION:<br />Various characters developed throughout the plot line. Rachel Brown did the most character development. At the beginning of the play, Rachel was fearful and emotionally beaten down by her oppressive father: Reverend Jeremiah Brown. He would control her by enforcing conservative beliefs on her. Furthermore, her relationship with the accused individual, Bertram Cates, put her in an impossible position. She loved Cates, but she did not agree with Cates’ progressive ideas. Also, she did not want to continue living by her father's fundamentalist ideas. Thankfully, after a series of events, she realized that what her father did to her was inevitable. She became a stronger woman after she realized that every individual has a right to his or her own thoughts, and every idea is worth being heard. <br /><br />Henry Drummond could be perceived as the protagonist in <i>Inherit the Wind</i>. He in a way was the hero of the play. Henry saved the townspeople from their close-minded fundamentalist views. Henry Drummond was a sophisticated man from the North who believed in freedom of thought. As a result, he agreed to defend Crates. Furthermore, he was undaunted by the townspeople bad behavior towards him. He was self-confident in the courtroom. Also, he was a static character. That was because all throughout the play, he defended the freedom of thought.<br /><br />IMPACT OF SETTING:<br />The setting greatly affected the occurrences in the book. To start off the play took place in 1925, so conservative viewpoints were the norm at the period. The Hillsboro community did not agree to thoughts or beliefs that did not support the <i> Bible's</i> perspectives. As a result, Cates’s teachings were not looked upon in a favorable way. They did not want their kids being influenced by tracks of thoughts that did not involve the <i>Bible’s</i> beliefs. If it weren’t for their close-minded customs, Cates would have never been placed in prison; therefore, none of the trial's complications would have happened.<br /><br />RECOMMENDATIONS:<br /> <i>Inherit the Wind</i> would be enjoyed best by students at a high school reading level due to the complex grammar used. The book also covers universal themes and concepts. For that reasoning, both genders would enjoy reading this book.<br />	<br /></span>

<div id="review_2674757426" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2674757426" />
    <a title="Jacob Norby" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59440653-jacob-norby"><img alt="Jacob Norby" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1520431423p2/59440653.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2674757426?book_show_action=true">Jan 15, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jacob Norby" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jacob Norby" href="/user/show/59440653-jacob-norby">Jacob Norby</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2674757426" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16104664772032151454"> Personal Response<br/>	Even though <i>Inherit the Wind</i> was written and takes place years ago, the issues that are brought up are still present in our world today. The way the play started out, I assumed it was going to have a one sided opinion, but as it went on I realized that was far from the truth. This drama supports the idea of people listening to others’ ideas and thinking for themselves, which I believe is a good ideology to teach. Overall, I’m glad that I was required to read this book. <br/><br/>Plot<br/>	In</span>
  <span id="freeText16104664772032151454" style="display:none"> Personal Response<br />	Even though <i>Inherit the Wind</i> was written and takes place years ago, the issues that are brought up are still present in our world today. The way the play started out, I assumed it was going to have a one sided opinion, but as it went on I realized that was far from the truth. This drama supports the idea of people listening to others’ ideas and thinking for themselves, which I believe is a good ideology to teach. Overall, I’m glad that I was required to read this book. <br /><br />Plot<br />	In the town of Dayton, Tennessee, a school teacher named Bertram Cates is arrested for teaching his students the theory of evolution. This violates a law called the Butler Act, which disallows the teaching of anything that goes against Christianity. For the court case, a famous attorney named Matthew Harrison Brady comes to town to prosecute against Bertram Cates. Brady is shown to be extremely influential on the town, and they welcome him with open arms. Soon they learn that another famous attorney is coming to town to defend Bertram Cates. His name is Henry Drummond, and the town is fearful he will be able to defeat Brady. The battle starts heating up and it begins to be uncertain on who will win the case. <br /><br />Characterization<br />	This book has a lot of characters in this book, but one of the most important is Matthew Harrison Brady. At the start he is shown to be an extremely arrogant man who thinks he can get his way just with his words, but he soon starts to break down once Henry Drummond begins to turn the town against him. Brady is revealed to be very close minded in his thinking, because he doesn’t even know anything about what he is fighting against.<br />	Another big character in this book is Henry Drummond. When Henry Drummond is first introduced, the town is terrified of him and compares him to the Devil. He becomes better respected when he proves his competence during the court case. Drummond is extremely open minded in his thinking; he considers every idea instead of focussing solely on one.<br />	The whole reason this story happens is because of Bertram Cates. He is a local school teacher who told his class about both sides, creationism and evolution. At first Cates simply wants to admit that he is guilty and end the court case, but Drummond convinces him that he should stand up for what he believes.<br /><br />Setting<br />	The book takes place in Hillsboro, Tennessee. The year it takes place in is 1925. The setting is very important because it takes place in the “Bible Belt”, meaning that the majority of the townsfolk are very religious. The time is also important because it is when the Butler Act was still a law.<br /><br /><br />Recommendation <br />	I’d recommend this play to anyone who hasn’t already read it. Its messages can be easily interpreted by anyone. I believe that the age demographic for this book is high schoolers and above, because I think anyone younger wouldn’t understand it. I also think any gender can read this book because the messages in it are pretty universal.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16104664772032151454" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('+HDW//jk4Dpi1UJtpBXNyiqul1lXWAx+OaqUt4FlzNpOHxWYir4zYYe8jg18hzC+TLgQfZ1rVP80saeOKXn31A==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  }

The play centers on Juror Eight, who is at first the sole holdout in an 11-1 guilty vote. Eight sets his sights not on proving the other jurors wrong but rather on getting them to look at the situation in a clear-eyed way not affected by their personal prejudices or biases. Reginald Rose deliberately and carefully peels away the layers of artifice from the men and allows a fuller picture to form of them—and of America, at its best and worst.   After the critically acclaimed teleplay aired in 1954, this landmark American drama went on to become a cinematic masterpiece in 1957 starring Henry Fonda, for which Rose wrote the adaptation. The popular image of the men who made the bomb is of dispassionate intellects who number-crunched their way towards a weapon whose devastating power they could not even imagine. But in his Tony Award-winning play Copenhagen, Michael Frayn shows us that these men were passionate, philosophical, and all too human, even though one of the three historical figures in his drama, Werner Heisenberg, was the head of the Nazis\' effort to develop a nuclear weapon. The play\'s other two characters, the Danish physicist Niels Bohr and his wife, Margrethe, are involved with Heisenberg in an after-death analysis of an actual meeting that has long puzzled historians. In 1941, the German scientist visited Bohr, his old mentor and long-time friend, in Copenhagen. After a brief discussion in the Bohrs\' home, the two men went for a short walk. What they discussed on that walk, and its implications for both scientists, have long been a mystery, even though both scientists gave (conflicting) accounts in later years. Frayn\'s cunning conceit is to use the scientific underpinnings of atomic physics, from Schr?dinger\'s famous cat to the Heisenberg uncertainty principle, to explore how an individual\'s point of view renders attempts to discover the ultimate truth of any human interaction fundamentally impossible. To Margrethe, Heisenberg was always an untrustworthy student, eager to steal from her husband\'s knowledge. To Bohr, Heisenberg was a brilliant if irresponsible foster son, whose lack of moral compass was part of his genius. As for Heisenberg, the man who could have built the bomb but somehow failed to, his dilemma is at the heart of the play\'s conflict. Frayn\'s clever dramatic structure, which returns repeatedly to particular scenes from different points of view, allows several possible theories as to what his motives could have been. In James Goldman’s classic play The Lion in Winter, domestic turmoil rises to an art form. Keenly self-aware and motivated as much by spite as by any sense of duty, Henry II and Eleanor of Aquitaine maneuver against each other to position their favorite son in line for succession. By imagining the inner lives of Henry, Eleanor, and their sons, John, Geoffrey, and Richard, Goldman created the quintessential drama of family strife and competing ambitions, a work that gives visceral, modern-day relevance to the intrigues of Angevin England. Combining keen historical and psychological insight with delicious, mordant wit, the stage play has become a touchstone of today’s theater scene, and Goldman’s screenplay for the 1968 film adaptation won him an Academy Award. Told in “marvelously articulate language, with humor that bristles and burns” (Los Angeles Times), The Lion in Winter is the rare play that bursts into life on the printed page.<\/span>\n  <a Based on historical people and real events, Miller\'s drama is a searing portrait of a community engulfed by hysteria. In the rigid theocracy of Salem, rumors that women are practicing witchcraft galvanize the town\'s most basic fears and suspicions; and when a young girl accuses Elizabeth Proctor of being a witch, self-righteous church leaders and townspeople insist that Elizabeth be brought to trial. The ruthlessness of the prosecutors and the eagerness of neighbor to testify against neighbor brilliantly illuminates the destructive power of socially sanctioned violence.\n\nWritten in 1953, The Crucible is a mirror Miller uses to reflect the anti-communist hysteria inspired by Senator Joseph McCarthy\'s &quot;witch-hunts&quot; in the United States. Within the text itself, Miller contemplates the parallels, writing, &quot;Political opposition... is given an inhumane overlay, which then justifies the abrogation of all normally applied customs of civilized behavior.
